  <title>Genome assembly and characterization of a complex zfBED-NLR gene-containing disease resistance locus in Carolina Gold Select rice with Nanopore sequencing</title>

  <link rel="canonical" href="https://journals.plos.org/plosgenetics/article?id=10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571" />
  <meta name="description" content="Author summary Plants lack adaptive immunity, and instead contain repeat-rich, disease resistance genes that evolve rapidly through duplication, recombination, and transposition. The number, variation, and often clustered arrangement of these genes make them challenging to sequence and catalog. The US heirloom rice variety Carolina Gold Select has resistance to two important bacterial diseases. Toward identifying the responsible gene(s), we combined long- and short-read sequencing technologies to assemble the whole genome and identify the resistance gene repertoire. We previously narrowed the location of the gene(s) to a region on chromosome four. The region in Carolina Gold Select is larger than in the rice reference genome (Nipponbare) and contains twice as many resistance genes. One shares unusual features with a known bacterial disease resistance gene, suggesting that it confers the resistance. Across diverse varieties and related species, we identified two widely-distributed groups of such genes. The results are an important step toward mechanistic characterization and deployment of the bacterial disease resistance. The genome assembly also identified a resistance gene for a fungal disease and predicted a marker phenotype used in breeding for resistance. Thus, the Carolina Gold Select genome assembly can be expected to aid in the identification and deployment of other valuable traits." />

  <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Long-read sequencing facilitates assembly of complex genomic regions. In plants, loci containing nucleotide-binding, leucine-rich repeat (NLR) disease resistance genes are an important example of such regions. NLR genes constitute one of the largest gene families in plants and are often clustered, evolving via duplication, contraction, and transposition. We recently mapped the Xo1 locus for resistance to bacterial blight and bacterial leaf streak, found in the American heirloom rice variety Carolina Gold Select, to a region that in the Nipponbare reference genome is NLR gene-rich. Here, toward identification of the Xo1 gene, we combined Nanopore and Illumina reads and generated a high-quality Carolina Gold Select genome assembly. We identified 529 complete or partial NLR genes and discovered, relative to Nipponbare, an expansion of NLR genes at the Xo1 locus. One of these has high sequence similarity to the cloned, functionally similar Xa1 gene. Both harbor an integrated zfBED domain, and the repeats within each protein are nearly perfect. Across diverse Oryzeae, we identified two sub-clades of NLR genes with these features, varying in the presence of the zfBED domain and the number of repeats. The Carolina Gold Select genome assembly also uncovered at the Xo1 locus a rice blast resistance gene and a gene encoding a polyphenol oxidase (PPO). PPO activity has been used as a marker for blast resistance at the locus in some varieties; however, the Carolina Gold Select sequence revealed a loss-of-function mutation in the PPO gene that breaks this association. Our results demonstrate that whole genome sequencing combining Nanopore and Illumina reads effectively resolves NLR gene loci. Our identification of an Xo1 candidate is an important step toward mechanistic characterization, including the role(s) of the zfBED domain. Finally, the Carolina Gold Select genome assembly will facilitate identification of other useful traits in this historically important variety.">


  <meta name="keywords" content="Genetic loci,Phylogenetic analysis,Genomics,Rice,Sequence alignment,Protein domains,Genome sequencing,Plant genomics" />

<div class="abstract toc-section abstract-type-"><a id="abstract0" name="abstract0" data-toc="abstract0" class="link-target" title="Abstract"></a><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="abstract-content"><a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Long-read sequencing facilitates assembly of complex genomic regions. In plants, loci containing nucleotide-binding, leucine-rich repeat (NLR) disease resistance genes are an important example of such regions. NLR genes constitute one of the largest gene families in plants and are often clustered, evolving via duplication, contraction, and transposition. We recently mapped the <em>Xo1</em> locus for resistance to bacterial blight and bacterial leaf streak, found in the American heirloom rice variety Carolina Gold Select, to a region that in the Nipponbare reference genome is NLR gene-rich. Here, toward identification of the <em>Xo1</em> gene, we combined Nanopore and Illumina reads and generated a high-quality Carolina Gold Select genome assembly. We identified 529 complete or partial NLR genes and discovered, relative to Nipponbare, an expansion of NLR genes at the <em>Xo1</em> locus. One of these has high sequence similarity to the cloned, functionally similar <em>Xa1</em> gene. Both harbor an integrated zfBED domain, and the repeats within each protein are nearly perfect. Across diverse Oryzeae, we identified two sub-clades of NLR genes with these features, varying in the presence of the zfBED domain and the number of repeats. The Carolina Gold Select genome assembly also uncovered at the <em>Xo1</em> locus a rice blast resistance gene and a gene encoding a polyphenol oxidase (PPO). PPO activity has been used as a marker for blast resistance at the locus in some varieties; however, the Carolina Gold Select sequence revealed a loss-of-function mutation in the PPO gene that breaks this association. Our results demonstrate that whole genome sequencing combining Nanopore and Illumina reads effectively resolves NLR gene loci. Our identification of an <em>Xo1</em> candidate is an important step toward mechanistic characterization, including the role(s) of the zfBED domain. Finally, the Carolina Gold Select genome assembly will facilitate identification of other useful traits in this historically important variety.</p>
</div></div><div class="abstract toc-section abstract-type-summary"><a id="abstract1" name="abstract1" data-toc="abstract1" class="link-target" title="Author summary"></a>
<h2>Author summary</h2>
<div class="abstract-content"><a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract2.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Plants lack adaptive immunity, and instead contain repeat-rich, disease resistance genes that evolve rapidly through duplication, recombination, and transposition. The number, variation, and often clustered arrangement of these genes make them challenging to sequence and catalog. The US heirloom rice variety Carolina Gold Select has resistance to two important bacterial diseases. Toward identifying the responsible gene(s), we combined long- and short-read sequencing technologies to assemble the whole genome and identify the resistance gene repertoire. We previously narrowed the location of the gene(s) to a region on chromosome four. The region in Carolina Gold Select is larger than in the rice reference genome (Nipponbare) and contains twice as many resistance genes. One shares unusual features with a known bacterial disease resistance gene, suggesting that it confers the resistance. Across diverse varieties and related species, we identified two widely-distributed groups of such genes. The results are an important step toward mechanistic characterization and deployment of the bacterial disease resistance. The genome assembly also identified a resistance gene for a fungal disease and predicted a marker phenotype used in breeding for resistance. Thus, the Carolina Gold Select genome assembly can be expected to aid in the identification and deployment of other valuable traits.</p>
</div></div>


<div class="articleinfo"><p><strong>Citation: </strong>Read AC, Moscou MJ, Zimin AV, Pertea G, Meyer RS, Purugganan MD, et al. <div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec001" name="sec001" data-toc="sec001" class="link-target" title="Introduction"></a><h2>Introduction</h2><a id="article1.body1.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Recent advances in sequencing technology enable the assembly of complex genomic loci by generating read lengths long enough to resolve repetitive regions [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref001" class="ref-tip">1</a>]. Repetitive regions are often hotspots of recombination and other genomic changes, but difficulties assembling them mean that they often remain as incomplete gaps for many years after a genome’s initial draft assembly. For example, the centromeres and telomeres remain unsequenced for nearly all plant and animal genomes today. The most straightforward way to span lengthy or complex repeats is to generate single reads that are longer than the repeats themselves, so that repeats can be placed in the correct genomic location. When repeats occur in tandem arrays, reads need to be longer than the entire array if one is to accurately determine the number of repeat copies that the array contains. One of the most promising current technologies for resolving complex repeats is nanopore-based sequencing from Oxford Nanopore Technologies ("Nanopore"), for which validated reads as long as 2,272,580 bases have been reported [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref002" class="ref-tip">2</a>], and improvements in base calling software are increasingly improving fidelity [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref003" class="ref-tip">3</a>]. Nanopore sequencing has been used for various applications, including genome sequencing of <em>Arabidopsis</em> and a wild tomato relative [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref004" class="ref-tip">4</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref005" class="ref-tip">5</a>], resolving complex T-DNA insertions [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref006" class="ref-tip">6</a>], and disease resistance gene enrichment sequencing [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref007" class="ref-tip">7</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Plant disease resistance loci represent an important example of complex portions of a genome that can be challenging to characterize in context using short-read sequencing. These loci often contain clusters of nucleotide binding leucine-rich repeat (NLR) protein genes. NLR proteins are structurally modular, typically containing an N-terminal coiled-coil (CC) domain or a Toll/interleukin-1 receptor (TIR) domain, a conserved nucleotide binding domain (NB-ARC), and a C-terminal region comprising a variable number of leucine-rich repeats (LRRs). The NLR gene family is one of the largest and most diverse in plants [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref008" class="ref-tip">8</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref009" class="ref-tip">9</a>], with 95, 151, and 458 members reported in the reference maize, <em>Arabidopsis</em>, and rice genomes, respectively [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref010" class="ref-tip">10</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref011" class="ref-tip">11</a>]. Fifty-one percent of the NLR genes occur in 44 clusters in the rice reference genome [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref012" class="ref-tip">12</a>]. Plants lack an adaptive immune system, and it has been theorized that this clustering provides plants an arsenal of resistance genes that can rapidly evolve, through duplication and recombination, to respond to dynamic pathogen populations [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>–<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref016" class="ref-tip">16</a>]. Indeed, the structure and content of NLR loci is variable, even in closely related cultivars, and, among plant populations, NLR genes account for the majority of copy-number and presence/absence polymorphisms [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref017" class="ref-tip">17</a>–<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>]. Adding to the complexity of NLR genes, and the challenge of their sequence assembly, is the recent observation that approximately 10% of NLR genes encode additional, non-canonical, integrated domains (IDs) that may act as decoys, have roles in oligomerization or downstream signaling [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref022" class="ref-tip">22</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref023" class="ref-tip">23</a>], or serve other functions. Analysis of closely related species has shown that these IDs appear to be modular, with independent integrations occurring in diverse NLR genes over evolutionary time [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref024" class="ref-tip">24</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>In this study, we used Nanopore long reads combined with Illumina short reads to generate a high quality, whole genome assembly of rice cultivar Carolina Gold Select. Using the assembly we sought to delineate NLR gene content with a focus on a disease resistance locus, <em>Xo1</em>, which we identified in this variety in 2016 [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref025" class="ref-tip">25</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Carolina Gold Select is a purified line of Carolina Gold, a long-grain variety known for its distinctive gold hull and nutty flavor. Carolina Gold was the dominant variety grown in colonial America and is rumored to have been imported from Madagascar in 1685. It is a breeding ancestor of many modern US varieties [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref026" class="ref-tip">26</a>]. Field production in the Carolinas stopped in 1927. McClung and Fjellstrom used trait data and molecular markers to produce the genetically uniform modern variety Carolina Gold Select in 2010 [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref026" class="ref-tip">26</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p5" name="article1.body1.sec1.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Genotyping and draft genome sequencing of Carolina Gold Select confirmed it to be in the tropical Japonica clade [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref028" class="ref-tip">28</a>], but have not been sufficient to resolve loci associated with important disease resistance phenotypes, such as <em>Xo1</em>. <em>Xo1</em> protects against two important bacterial diseases, bacterial leaf streak (BLS) and bacterial blight (BB), caused by <em>Xanthomonas oryzae</em> pv. oryzicola (Xoc) and <em>X</em>. <em>oryzae</em> pv. oryzae (Xoo), respectively. It maps to a 1.09 Mb region of the long arm of chromosome four and segregates as a single dominant locus [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref025" class="ref-tip">25</a>]. The <em>Xo1</em> locus overlaps several mapped loci for resistance to BB, including <em>Xa1</em>, <em>Xa2</em>, <em>Xa12</em>, <em>Xa14</em>, <em>Xa17</em>, <em>Xa31(t)</em>, and <em>Xa38</em>, that have been isolated from various rice cultivars [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref029" class="ref-tip">29</a>–<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref036" class="ref-tip">36</a>]. Of these, only <em>Xa1</em> has been cloned, and it encodes an NLR with an N-terminal, integrated zinc-finger BED [zfBED; 37] domain and highly conserved, tandem repeats in the LRR region [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref038" class="ref-tip">38</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p6" name="article1.body1.sec1.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>Though the molecular mechanism is not yet known, <em>Xo1</em> resistance is elicited by any of the ~20 transcription activator-like (TAL) effectors injected into the plant cell by any given Xoc or Xoo strain. TAL effectors are modular, type III-secreted, sequence-specific DNA binding proteins that directly transcriptionally activate host genes (see [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref039" class="ref-tip">39</a>] for a review). They each are made up of an N-terminal type III secretion signal, a central DNA binding domain, and, in the C-terminal region, nuclear localization signals and an acidic activation domain. TAL effectors differ from one another, within and often across strains, in their DNA-binding specificity and the gene(s) they activate. Several have been identified that activate host genes that contribute to disease development [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref040" class="ref-tip">40</a>]. A given TAL effector may trigger host resistance if it transcriptionally activates a so-called "executor resistance gene" [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref041" class="ref-tip">41</a>]. Such genes are distinct from NLR genes, and their expression alone is sufficient for death of the host cell and effective defense against further invasion by the pathogen. Resistance triggered by a TAL effector independent of its ability to activate a gene is rarer, reported to date only for the pepper TIR-NLR protein Bs4 [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref042" class="ref-tip">42</a>] and, more recently, the products of the <em>Xo1</em> locus and <em>Xa1</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref025" class="ref-tip">25</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref043" class="ref-tip">43</a>]. Interestingly, TAL effector-triggered, <em>Xo1</em>- and <em>Xa1</em>-mediated resistance is suppressed by pathogen delivery of N- and C-terminally truncated TAL effector proteins, called truncTALEs or iTALs, that are found (so far) exclusively in Asian strains of Xoo and Xoc [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref025" class="ref-tip">25</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref043" class="ref-tip">43</a>,<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref044" class="ref-tip">44</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p7" name="article1.body1.sec1.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>Based on the functional similarity of <em>Xo1</em> to <em>Xa1</em>, and to <em>Bs4</em>, and the fact that the region corresponding to the <em>Xo1</em> locus in the rice reference genome (IRGSP-1.0; cv. Nipponbare, which lacks the BLS and BB resistance) [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref045" class="ref-tip">45</a>] contains an array of seven NLR genes similar to each other (suggesting the potential for rapid evolution), we hypothesized that <em>Xo1</em>-mediated resistance in Carolina Gold Select is conferred by an NLR gene at the <em>Xo1</em> locus. The Carolina Gold Select genome assembly revealed fourteen such genes at the locus, including a candidate highly similar but not identical to <em>Xa1</em>, encoding an N-terminal, integrated zfBED domain and highly conserved, C-terminal, tandem repeats. In addition to the whole genome assembly, we present a detailed structural and comparative analysis of the <em>Xo1</em> candidate and other NLR genes at the <em>Xo1</em> locus, and an examination of zfBED-NLR gene content overall across representative species in the tribe Oryzeae.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec002" name="sec002" data-toc="sec002" class="link-target" title="Results and discussion"></a><h2>Results and discussion</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec003" name="sec003" class="link-target" title="Carolina Gold Select genome assembly and annotation"></a>
<h3>Carolina Gold Select genome assembly and annotation</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>To generate an assembly made up of large contigs with low error-rate, several assembly methods were used. We found that assembly by Flye [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref046" class="ref-tip">46</a>] using only Nanopore data yielded long contigs but a high consensus error rate. MaSuRCA [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref047" class="ref-tip">47</a>] assembly using both Illumina and Nanopore reads contained more sequence and had a very low consensus error rate, less than 1 error per 10,000 bases. We assessed the completeness of each assembly by aligning it to the Nipponbare reference genome using nucmer [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref048" class="ref-tip">48</a>] with default parameters. The Flye and MaSuRCA assemblies covered 92% and 93% of the reference respectively. Combining the two assemblies resulted in a reconciled Carolina Gold Select assembly that benefited from both the higher quality consensus sequence and completeness of the MaSuRCA assembly, and the greater contiguity of the Flye assembly. <a href="#pgen-1008571-t001">Table 1</a> lists the quantitative statistics of both assemblies as well as the reconciled assembly. For N50 computations, we used a genome size estimate of 377,689,190 bp, equal to the total size of scaffolds of the final reconciled assembly.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008571-t001" name="pgen-1008571-t001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t001</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>We found that the Carolina Gold Select assembly aligned to the Nipponbare reference genome with an average identity of 98.96%. 350,765,472 bases of the assembly (93%) aligned to 347,609,898 bases (93%) of the reference. The chromosome scaffolding process found 29 breaks in the scaffolds that were apparent mis-assemblies, and these were resolved. We call the final chromosomes Carolina_Gold_Select_1.0. <a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008571-t002" name="pgen-1008571-t002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.t002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

For the 12 chromosomes, our mapping process identified and annotated 80,753 gene loci, of which 33,818 have protein coding transcripts. We identified a total of 86,983 transcripts, of which 40,047 are protein coding and have identified CDS features. The total number of bases covered by exons is 52,082,180 bp, or 14.2% of the total length of all 12 chromosomes, whose lengths sum to 366,055,270 bp.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec004" name="sec004" class="link-target" title="NLR genes in the Carolina Gold Select assembly"></a>
<h3>NLR genes in the Carolina Gold Select assembly</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>To identify NLR genes in the Carolina Gold Select genome, we used NLR-Annotator, an expanded version of the NLR-Parser tool [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref049" class="ref-tip">49</a>]. NLR-Annotator does not rely on annotation data and does not mask repetitive regions, facilitating an unbiased analysis of the complete genome including NLR genes [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref050" class="ref-tip">50</a>]. Because the NLR-Annotator pipeline has not been validated in rice, we first ran the pipeline on the well-annotated Nipponbare reference. A total of 518 complete or partial NLR genes were predicted. The list of 518 NLR genes is an overestimation of the number of true Nipponbare NLR genes in part because it includes complete and partial NLR genes, some of which are classified as pseudogenes due to the presence of a stop codon in a predicted coding sequence (<a href="#pgen-1008571-g001">Fig 1A</a>). Genomic locations of the 518 Nipponbare NLR genes were cross-referenced with a list of 360 annotated Nipponbare NLR genes included in a recent analysis [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref024" class="ref-tip">24</a>]; 356 matched. Of the four Nipponbare NLR genes that were not identified by NLR-Annotator, one lacks one or more canonical NLR gene domains based on InterProScan predictions. The other three appear to be complete, however, indicating an overall NLR-Annotator detection success rate of 99.2% (<a href="#pgen.1008571.s001">S1 Table</a>). NLR-Annotator identified some complete NLR genes in the Nipponbare genome distinct from the 356; these may represent previously undetected NLR genes, pseudogenes, or false positives (highlighted in <a href="#pgen.1008571.s001">S1 Table</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008571-g001" name="pgen-1008571-g001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 1. </span> NLR proteins encoded in Carolina Gold Select in relation to Nipponbare and selected <em>R</em> genes.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(a) Number and chromosomal distribution of all NLR-Annotator predicted NLR genes in Carolina Gold Select and Nipponbare assemblies. ‘Pseudo’, predicted NLR genes with stop codons in any domain. ‘Partial’, predicted NLR genes missing a canonical domain. All NLR gene types are included in order to provide a high-level comparison of NLR distribution in the two assemblies. (b) Maximum likelihood tree of encoded NB-ARC domains of NLR genes in Carolina Gold Select and Nipponbare, as predicted by NLR-Annotator. Incomplete NLR genes and genes with a stop codon in the NB-ARC domain are not included in the phylogeny. Sixteen cloned resistance genes are included for reference. Branches with bootstrap support greater than 80 percent are indicated with pink squares. Interactive tree available at <a href="http://itol.embl.de/shared/acr242">http://itol.embl.de/shared/acr242</a>. NB-ARC domain sequences available in <a href="#pgen.1008571.s003">S3 Table</a>. (c) Examples of expansion (top), contraction (middle) and transposition (bottom) of NLR genes in Carolina Gold Select relative to Nipponbare. Bootstrap values greater than 80 percent are displayed. Further details available in <a href="#pgen.1008571.s004">S4 Table</a>. In the example of expansion at the <em>Xo1</em> locus, as described in the text, CGS chr4 nlr9 is <em>CGS-Xo1</em><sub><em>1</em></sub>, CGS chr4 nlr10 is <em>CGS-Xo1</em><sub><em>2</em></sub>, CGS chr4 nlr12 is <em>CGS-Xo1</em><sub><em>4</em></sub>, and Nb chr4 nlr16 is <em>Nb-xo1</em><sub><em>1</em></sub>.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g001</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Running the Carolina Gold Select assembly through the NLR-Annotator pipeline identified 529 total NLR genes. The Carolina Gold Select NLR genes are organized similarly to those of Nipponbare, occurring irregularly across the 12 chromosomes, with a large proportion occurring on chromosome 11 (<a href="#pgen-1008571-g001">Fig 1A</a> and <a href="#pgen.1008571.s002">S2 Table</a>). This similarity in number and genomic distribution of NLR genes provides support for the integrity of the Carolina Gold Select genome assembly.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>To determine relationships between and among Nipponbare and Carolina Gold Select NLR genes, a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree was generated using amino acid sequences of the central NB-ARC domain for all complete and complete pseudo- NLR genes, except those in which the NB-ARC domain is interrupted by a stop codon or has gaps greater than 50% across the alignment. NB-ARC domains from 16 cloned, NLR-type, rice resistance genes (<a href="#pgen.1008571.s003">S3 Table</a>) were included to identify potential orthologs in Carolina Gold Select. Although the total number of predicted NLR genes is similar between the two cultivars, the resulting tree revealed 26 expansions and 37 contractions within NLR gene clades in Carolina Gold Select relative to Nipponbare, as well as 3 transpositions and 6 examples of combinations of transposition and either expansion or contraction (<a href="#pgen-1008571-g001">Fig 1B</a>, <a href="#pgen-1008571-g001">Fig 1C</a>, and <a href="#pgen.1008571.s004">S4 Table</a>). Seven of the cloned resistance genes (<em>Pib</em>, <em>Pik2</em>, <em>Pi63</em>, <em>Pi2</em>, <em>RGA5</em>, <em>Pi36</em>, and <em>Pi37</em>) group with expanded or contracted NLR gene clades. The observed differences in NLR gene content in the two closely related cultivars is consistent with previous comparative analyses demonstrating that NLR gene families evolve rapidly and are characterized by presence-absence variation [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref019" class="ref-tip">19</a>–<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>].</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec005" name="sec005" class="link-target" title="Expansion at the Carolina Gold Select Xo1 locus"></a>
<h3>Expansion at the Carolina Gold Select <em>Xo1</em> locus</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>We extracted the region of the Carolina Gold Select assembly that corresponds to the 1.09 Mb Nipponbare <em>Xo1</em> mapping interval [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref025" class="ref-tip">25</a>] and found that it spans a much larger region, 1.30 Mb, that includes a 182 kb insertion (<a href="#pgen-1008571-g002">Fig 2</a>). It is unclear if this relative expansion is unique to a particular subgroup of <em>O</em>. <em>sativa</em> cultivars, but it is not present in the long-read (PacBio) assembly of <em>O</em>. <em>sativa</em> indica cultivar IR8 (<a href="#pgen.1008571.s012">S1 Fig</a>) [<a href="#pgen.1008571.ref051" class="ref-tip">51</a>]. Hereafter, we refer to the region in Nipponbare, which as noted lacks the resistance to BLS and BB, as <em>Nb-xo1</em> and to the region in Carolina Gold Select as <em>CGS-Xo1</em>.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008571-g002" name="pgen-1008571-g002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008571.g002">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 2. </span> Expansion at the Carolina Gold Select <em>Xo1</em> locus and identification of an <em>Xo1</em> candidate.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(a) Comparison of the <em>Xo1</em> locus in Carolina Gold Select and in Nipponbare. Areas of darker color on the two cartoon loci connected by gray shading represent regions of high similarity. Triangles indicate positions of NLR genes predicted by NLR-Annotator, designated from left to right as <em>CGS-Xo1</em><sub><em>1</em></sub> through <em>CGS-Xo1</em><sub><em>14</em></sub> in Carolina Gold Select and <em>Nb-xo1</em><sub><em>1</em></sub> through <em>Nb-xo1</em><sub><em>7</em></sub> in Nipponbare.
